<p><b>Jerome David "J. D." Salinger</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="/dʒ/ 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted" title="/ər/ 'er' in 'finger'">ər</span></a></span><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span>; January 1, 1919 –  January 27, 2010) was an <a href="/wiki/American_literature" title="American literature">American writer</a> who won acclaim early in life. He led a very private life for more than a half-century. He published his final original work in 1965 and gave his last interview in 1980.</p>
<p>Salinger was raised in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> and began writing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_stories" title="Short stories">short stories</a> while in secondary school. Several were published in <i><a href="/wiki/Story_(magazine)" title="Story (magazine)">Story</a></i> magazine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> in the early 1940s before he began serving in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. In 1948, his critically acclaimed story "<a href="/wiki/A_Perfect_Day_for_Bananafish" title="A Perfect Day for Bananafish">A Perfect Day for Bananafish</a>" appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> magazine, which became home to much of his later work. In 1951, his novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye">The Catcher in the Rye</a></i> was an immediate popular success. His depiction of adolescent alienation and loss of innocence in the protagonist <a href="/wiki/Holden_Caulfield" title="Holden Caulfield">Holden Caulfield</a> was influential, especially among adolescent readers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sonny_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-sonny-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The novel remains widely read and controversial,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> selling around 250,000 copies a year.</p>
<p>The success of <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> led to public attention and scrutiny. Salinger became reclusive, publishing new work less frequently. He followed <i>Catcher</i> with a short story collection, <i><a href="/wiki/Nine_Stories_(Salinger)" title="Nine Stories (Salinger)">Nine Stories</a></i> (1953); a volume containing a novella and a short story, <i><a href="/wiki/Franny_and_Zooey" title="Franny and Zooey">Franny and Zooey</a></i> (1961); and a volume containing two novellas, <i><a href="/wiki/Raise_High_the_Roof_Beam,_Carpenters_and_Seymour:_An_Introduction" title="Raise High the Roof Beam, Carpenters and Seymour: An Introduction">Raise High the Roof Beam, Carpenters and Seymour: An Introduction</a></i> (1963). His last published work, a novella entitled "<a href="/wiki/Hapworth_16,_1924" title="Hapworth 16, 1924">Hapworth 16, 1924</a>", appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> on June 19, 1965.</p>
<p>Afterward, Salinger struggled with unwanted attention, including a legal battle in the 1980s with biographer <a href="/wiki/Ian_Hamilton_(critic)" title="Ian Hamilton (critic)">Ian Hamilton</a> and the release in the late 1990s of memoirs written by two people close to him: <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Maynard" title="Joyce Maynard">Joyce Maynard</a>, an ex-lover; and Margaret Salinger, his daughter. In 1996, a small publisher announced a deal with Salinger to publish "Hapworth 16, 1924" in book form, but amid the ensuing publicity the release was indefinitely delayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> He made headlines around the globe in June 2009 when he filed a lawsuit against another writer for copyright infringement resulting from that writer's use of one of the characters from <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger died of natural causes on January 27, 2010, at his home in <a href="/wiki/Cornish,_New_Hampshire" title="Cornish, New Hampshire">Cornish, New Hampshire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCNews_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCNews-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeathABC_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeathABC-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-death-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> In November 2013, three unpublished stories by Salinger were briefly posted online. One of the stories, called "<a href="/wiki/The_Ocean_Full_of_Bowling_Balls" title="The Ocean Full of Bowling Balls">The Ocean Full of Bowling Balls</a>," is said to be a prequel to <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_experiences">Early life and experiences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and experiences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Jerome David Salinger was born in New York City, on New Year's Day, 1919. His mother, Marie (née Jillich), was born in <a href="/wiki/Atlantic,_Iowa" title="Atlantic, Iowa">Atlantic</a>, Iowa, of Scottish, German, and Irish descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Salinger_2007_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-D._Salinger_2007-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> His paternal grandfather, Simon, born in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, was at one time the rabbi for the <a href="/wiki/Adath_Jeshurun_Congregation" title="Adath Jeshurun Congregation">Adath Jeshurun congregation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Louisville,_Kentucky" title="Louisville, Kentucky">Louisville</a>, Kentucky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> His father, Sol Salinger, sold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosher" title="Kosher">kosher</a> cheese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's mother changed her name to Miriam and passed as Jewish and never considered herself, after marrying Salinger’s father, anything but Jewish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger did not learn that his mother was not Jewish until just after his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bar_mitzvah" title="Bar mitzvah">bar mitzvah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> His only sibling was his older sister Doris (1911–2001).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As a youth, Salinger attended public schools on the <a href="/wiki/West_Side_(Manhattan)" title="West Side (Manhattan)">West Side of Manhattan</a>. Then in 1932, the family moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Park_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Park Avenue (Manhattan)">Park Avenue</a>, and Salinger was enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/McBurney_School" title="McBurney School">McBurney School</a>, a nearby private school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Salinger_2007_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-D._Salinger_2007-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> At McBurney, he managed the fencing team, wrote for the school newspaper and appeared in plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Salinger_2007_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-D._Salinger_2007-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> He "showed an innate talent for drama", though his father opposed the idea of J.D.'s becoming an actor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3595" data-file-width="2994" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg/220px-1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg/330px-1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg/440px-1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1133_Park_Avenue_cloudy_jeh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1133 Park Avenue</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Salinger had trouble fitting in at his new school and took measures to conform, such as calling himself Jerry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lutz.2C_Norma_Jean_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lutz.2C_Norma_Jean-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> (His family called him Sonny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hathcock.2C_Barrett_2010_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hathcock.2C_Barrett_2010-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup>)</p>
<p>His parents then enrolled him at <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge_Military_Academy_and_College" title="Valley Forge Military Academy and College">Valley Forge Military Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wayne,_Pennsylvania" title="Wayne, Pennsylvania">Wayne, Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Salinger_2007_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-D._Salinger_2007-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Though he had written for the school newspaper at McBurney, Salinger began writing stories "under the covers [at night], with the aid of a flashlight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger was the literary editor of the class yearbook, <i>Cross Sabres</i>. He also participated in the Glee Club, Aviation Club, French Club, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staff_Noncommissioned_Officer" title="Staff Noncommissioned Officer">Non-Commissioned Officers</a> Club.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lutz.2C_Norma_Jean_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lutz.2C_Norma_Jean-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's Valley Forge <a href="/wiki/201_file" title="201 file">201 file</a> reveals that he was a "mediocre" student, and unlike the <a href="/wiki/Overachievement" title="Overachievement">overachievement</a> enjoyed by members of the <a href="/wiki/Glass_family" title="Glass family">Glass family</a> he would go on to write about, his recorded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IQ" title="IQ">IQ</a> was far from that of a <a href="/wiki/Genius" title="Genius">genius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> He graduated in 1936.</p>
<p>Salinger started his freshman year at <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a> in 1936. He considered studying <a href="/wiki/Special_education" title="Special education">special education</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> but dropped out the following spring. That fall, his father urged him to learn about the meat-importing business, and he went to work at a company in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, Austria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thirtynine_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-thirtynine-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> He left Austria one month before it was <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">annexed by Nazi Germany</a> on March 12, 1938.</p>
<p>In the fall of 1938, Salinger attended <a href="/wiki/Ursinus_College" title="Ursinus College">Ursinus College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Collegeville,_Pennsylvania" title="Collegeville, Pennsylvania">Collegeville</a>, Pennsylvania, and wrote a column called "skipped diploma", which included movie reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> He dropped out after one semester.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Salinger_2007_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-D._Salinger_2007-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hathcock.2C_Barrett_2010_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hathcock.2C_Barrett_2010-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1939, Salinger attended a <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_School_of_General_Studies" title="Columbia University School of General Studies">Columbia University School of General Studies</a> evening writing class taught by <a href="/wiki/Whit_Burnett" title="Whit Burnett">Whit Burnett</a>, longtime editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Story_(magazine)" title="Story (magazine)">Story</a></i> magazine. According to Burnett, Salinger did not distinguish himself until a few weeks before the end of the second semester, at which point "he suddenly came to life" and completed three stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burnett_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-burnett-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Burnett told Salinger that his stories were skillful and accomplished, accepting "<a href="/wiki/The_Young_Folks" title="The Young Folks">The Young Folks</a>", a <a href="/wiki/Vignette_(literature)" title="Vignette (literature)">vignette</a> about several aimless youths, for publication in <i>Story</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burnett_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-burnett-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's debut short story was published in the magazine's March–April 1940 issue. Burnett became Salinger's mentor, and they corresponded for several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lutz.2C_Norma_Jean_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lutz.2C_Norma_Jean-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1941, Salinger started dating <a href="/wiki/Oona_O%27Neill" title="Oona O'Neill">Oona O'Neill</a>, daughter of the playwright <a href="/wiki/Eugene_O%27Neill" title="Eugene O'Neill">Eugene O'Neill</a>. Despite finding the debutante self-absorbed (he confided to a friend that "Little Oona's hopelessly in love with little Oona"), he called her often and wrote her long letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oona_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-oona-27"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Their relationship ended when Oona began seeing <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>, whom she eventually married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trespass_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-trespass-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> In late 1941, Salinger briefly worked on a <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ship</a>, serving as an activity director and possibly as a performer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cruise_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-cruise-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The same year, Salinger began submitting short stories to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>. Seven of Salinger's stories were rejected by the magazine that year, including "Lunch for Three", "Monologue for a Watery Highball", and "I Went to School with Adolf Hitler". In December 1941, however, the publication accepted "<a href="/wiki/Slight_Rebellion_off_Madison" title="Slight Rebellion off Madison">Slight Rebellion off Madison</a>", a Manhattan-set story about a disaffected teenager named <a href="/wiki/Holden_Caulfield" title="Holden Caulfield">Holden Caulfield</a> with "pre-war jitters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yagoda_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-yagoda-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> When Japan carried out the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a> that month, the story was rendered "unpublishable"; it did not appear in the magazine until 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yagoda_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-yagoda-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> In the spring of 1942, several months after the United States entered <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Salinger was <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_States" title="Conscription in the United States">drafted</a> into the army, wherein he saw combat with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._12th_Infantry_Regiment" title="U.S. 12th Infantry Regiment">12th Infantry Regiment</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._4th_Infantry_Division" title="U.S. 4th Infantry Division">4th Infantry Division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cruise_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-cruise-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> He was active at <a href="/wiki/Utah_Beach" title="Utah Beach">Utah Beach</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-Day" title="D-Day">D-Day</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bulge" title="Battle of the Bulge">Battle of the Bulge</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_H%C3%BCrtgen_Forest" title="Battle of Hürtgen Forest">Battle of Hürtgen Forest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the campaign from Normandy into Germany, Salinger arranged to meet with <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>, a writer who had influenced him and was then working as a war correspondent in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger was impressed with Hemingway's friendliness and modesty, finding him more "soft" than his gruff public persona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-baker-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Hemingway was impressed by Salinger's writing and remarked: "Jesus, he has a helluva talent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sonny_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-sonny-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The two writers began corresponding; Salinger wrote Hemingway in July 1946 that their talks were among his few positive memories of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-baker-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger added that he was working on a play about Holden Caulfield, the protagonist of his story "Slight Rebellion off Madison", and hoped to play the part himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baker_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-baker-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Salinger was assigned to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter-intelligence" title="Counter-intelligence">counter-intelligence</a> division, for which he used his proficiency in French and German to interrogate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fiff_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-fiff-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> In April 1945 he entered a liberated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentration_camp" title="Concentration camp">concentration camp</a>, probably one of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dachau_Concentration_Camp" title="Dachau Concentration Camp">Dachau</a>'s sub-camps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fiff_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-fiff-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger earned the rank of Staff Sergeant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> and served in five campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's experiences in the war affected him emotionally. He was hospitalized for a few weeks for <a href="/wiki/Combat_stress_reaction" title="Combat stress reaction">combat stress reaction</a> after Germany was defeated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> and he later told his daughter: "You never really get the smell of burning flesh out of your nose entirely, no matter how long you live."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fiff_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-fiff-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Both of his biographers speculate that Salinger drew upon his wartime experiences in several stories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-at_fifty_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-at_fifty-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> such as "<a href="/wiki/For_Esm%C3%A9%E2%80%94with_Love_and_Squalor" title="For Esmé—with Love and Squalor">For Esmé—with Love and Squalor</a>", which is narrated by a traumatized soldier. Salinger continued to write while serving in the army, publishing several stories in slick magazines such as <i><a href="/wiki/Collier%27s" title="Collier's">Collier's</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Saturday_Evening_Post" title="The Saturday Evening Post">The Saturday Evening Post</a></i>. He also continued to submit stories to <i>The New Yorker</i>, but with little success; it rejected all of his submissions from 1944 to 1946, a group of 15 poems in 1945 alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yagoda_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-yagoda-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_years">Post-war years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Post-war years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After Germany's defeat, Salinger signed up for a six-month period of "<a href="/wiki/Denazification" title="Denazification">Denazification</a>" duty in Germany<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> for the <a href="/wiki/Counterintelligence_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Counterintelligence Corps (United States Army)">Counterintelligence Corps</a>. He lived in <a href="/wiki/Wei%C3%9Fenburg_in_Bayern" title="Weißenburg in Bayern">Weissenburg</a> and, soon after, married Sylvia Welter. He brought her to the United States in April 1946, but the marriage fell apart after eight months and Sylvia returned to Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thir_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-thir-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1972, Salinger's daughter Margaret was with him when he received a letter from Sylvia. He looked at the envelope, and without reading it, tore it apart. It was the first time he had heard from her since the breakup, but as Margaret put it, "when he was finished with a person, he was through with them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1946, Whit Burnett agreed to help Salinger publish a collection of his short stories through <i>Story</i> Press's Lippincott Imprint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-young-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Titled <i>The Young Folks</i>, the collection was to consist of twenty stories—ten, like the title story and "Slight Rebellion off Madison", were already in print; ten were previously unpublished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-young-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Though Burnett implied the book would be published and even negotiated Salinger a $1,000 advance on its sale, Lippincott overruled Burnett and rejected the book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-young-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger blamed Burnett for the book's failure to see print, and the two became estranged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>By the late 1940s, Salinger had become an avid follower of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zen_Buddhism" title="Zen Buddhism">Zen Buddhism</a>, to the point that he "gave reading lists on the subject to his dates"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sonny_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-sonny-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> and arranged a meeting with Buddhist scholar <a href="/wiki/D._T._Suzuki" title="D. T. Suzuki">D. T. Suzuki</a>.</p>
<p>In 1947, the author submitted a short story titled simply "The Bananafish" to <i>The New Yorker</i>. William Maxwell, the magazine's fiction editor, was impressed enough with "the singular quality of the story" that the magazine asked Salinger to continue revising it. He spent a year reworking it with New Yorker editors and the magazine accepted the story, now titled "<a href="/wiki/A_Perfect_Day_for_Bananafish" title="A Perfect Day for Bananafish">A Perfect Day for Bananafish</a>", and published it in the January 31, 1948 issue. The magazine thereon offered Salinger a "first-look" contract that allowed them <a href="/wiki/Right_of_first_refusal" title="Right of first refusal">right of first refusal</a> on any future stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The critical acclaim accorded "Bananafish", coupled with problems Salinger had with stories being altered by the "slicks", led him to publish almost exclusively in <i>The New Yorker</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> "Bananafish" was also the first of Salinger's published stories to feature the <a href="/wiki/Glass_family" title="Glass family">Glasses</a>, a fictional family consisting of two retired <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a> performers and their seven precocious children: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seymour_Glass" title="Seymour Glass">Seymour</a>, Buddy, Boo Boo, Walt, Waker, Zooey, and Franny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glass_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-glass-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger eventually published seven stories about the Glasses, developing a detailed family history and focusing particularly on Seymour, the brilliant but troubled eldest child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glass_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-glass-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1940s, Salinger had confided in a letter to Whit Burnett that he was eager to sell the film rights to some of his stories in order to achieve financial security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holly_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-holly-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Ian Hamilton, Salinger was disappointed when "rumblings from Hollywood" over his 1943 short story "<a href="/wiki/The_Varioni_Brothers" title="The Varioni Brothers">The Varioni Brothers</a>" came to nothing. Therefore he immediately agreed when, in mid-1948, independent film producer <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Goldwyn" title="Samuel Goldwyn">Samuel Goldwyn</a> offered to buy the film rights to his short story "<a href="/wiki/Uncle_Wiggily_in_Connecticut" title="Uncle Wiggily in Connecticut">Uncle Wiggily in Connecticut</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holly_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-holly-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Though Salinger sold his story with the hope—in the words of his agent Dorothy Olding—that it "would make a good movie",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> the film version of "Wiggily" was lambasted by critics upon its release in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berg_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-berg-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Renamed <i><a href="/wiki/My_Foolish_Heart_(film)" title="My Foolish Heart (film)">My Foolish Heart</a></i> and starring <a href="/wiki/Dana_Andrews" title="Dana Andrews">Dana Andrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Susan_Hayward" title="Susan Hayward">Susan Hayward</a>, the melodramatic film departed to such an extent from Salinger's story that Goldwyn biographer <a href="/wiki/A._Scott_Berg" title="A. Scott Berg">A. Scott Berg</a> referred to it as a "<a href="/wiki/Corruption_(linguistics)" title="Corruption (linguistics)">bastardization</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berg_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-berg-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result of this experience, Salinger never again permitted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_adaptations" title="Film adaptations">film adaptations</a> to be made from his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-depos_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-depos-52"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Brigitte_Bardot" title="Brigitte Bardot">Brigitte Bardot</a> wanted to buy the rights to "<a href="/wiki/A_Perfect_Day_for_Bananafish" title="A Perfect Day for Bananafish">A Perfect Day for Bananafish</a>", Salinger refused the request, but told his friend, <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Ross_(journalist)" title="Lillian Ross (journalist)">Lillian Ross</a>, longtime staff writer for <i>The New Yorker</i>, "She's a cute, talented, lost <i>enfante</i>, and I'm tempted to accommodate her, <i>pour le sport</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross2010_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ross2010-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Catcher_in_the_Rye"><i>The Catcher in the Rye</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Catcher in the Rye">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye">The Catcher in the Rye</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1373" data-file-width="837" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg/170px-Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg/255px-Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg/340px-Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catcher-in-the-rye-red-cover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cover of <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> 1985 edition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1940s, Salinger confided to several people that he was working on a novel featuring Holden Caulfield, the teenage protagonist of his short story "Slight Rebellion off Madison",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye">The Catcher in the Rye</a></i> was published on July 16, 1951, by Little, Brown and Company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> The novel's plot is simple,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simp_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-simp-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> detailing 16-year-old Holden's experiences in New York City following his expulsion and departure from an elite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_preparatory_school" title="College preparatory school">college preparatory school</a>. Not only was he expelled from his current school, he had also been expelled from three previous schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The book is more notable for the persona and testimonial voice of its <a href="/wiki/First-person_narrative" title="First-person narrative">first-person narrator</a>, Holden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nandel_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-nandel-58"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> He serves as an insightful but <a href="/wiki/Unreliable_narrator" title="Unreliable narrator">unreliable narrator</a> who expounds on the importance of loyalty, the "phoniness" of adulthood, and his own duplicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nandel_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-nandel-58"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> In a 1953 interview with a high school newspaper, Salinger admitted that the novel was "sort of" autobiographical, explaining, "My boyhood was very much the same as that of the boy in the book ... [I]t was a great relief telling people about it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Initial reactions to the book were mixed, ranging from <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> hailing <i>Catcher</i> as "an unusually brilliant first novel"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> to denigrations of the book's monotonous language and the "immorality and perversion" of Holden,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-virginia_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-virginia-61"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> who uses religious slurs and freely discusses casual sex and prostitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The novel was a popular success; within two months of its publication, <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> had been reprinted eight times. It spent 30 weeks on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Bestseller" title="New York Times Bestseller"><i>New York Times</i> Bestseller list</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simp_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-simp-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The book's initial success was followed by a brief lull in popularity, but by the late 1950s, according to Ian Hamilton, it had "become the book all brooding adolescents had to buy, the indispensable manual from which cool styles of disaffectation could be borrowed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cult_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-cult-63"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> It has been compared to <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Newspapers began publishing articles about the "Catcher Cult",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cult_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-cult-63"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> and the novel was banned in several countries—as well as some U.S. schools—because of its subject matter and what <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_World" title="Catholic World">Catholic World</a></i> reviewer Riley Hughes called an "excessive use of amateur swearing and coarse language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swearing_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-swearing-65"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> One diligent parent counted 237 appearances of the word "goddam" in the novel, along with 58 of "bastard", 31 of "Chrissake" and six of "fuck".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swearing_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-swearing-65"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1970s, several U.S. high school teachers who assigned the book were fired or forced to resign. A 1979 study of censorship noted that <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> "had the dubious distinction of being at once the most frequently censored book across the nation and the second-most frequently taught novel in public high schools" (after <a href="/wiki/John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck">John Steinbeck</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Of_Mice_and_Men" title="Of Mice and Men">Of Mice and Men</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cens_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-cens-66"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> The book remains widely read; in 2004, the novel was selling about 250,000 copies per year, "with total worldwide sales over 10 million copies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the wake of its 1950s success, Salinger received (and rejected) numerous offers to adapt <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> for the screen, including one from Samuel Goldwyn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berg_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-berg-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Since its publication, there has been sustained interest in the novel among filmmakers, with <a href="/wiki/Billy_Wilder" title="Billy Wilder">Billy Wilder</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Weinstein" title="Harvey Weinstein">Harvey Weinstein</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-post-69"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> among those seeking to secure the rights. Salinger stated in the 1970s that "<a href="/wiki/Jerry_Lewis" title="Jerry Lewis">Jerry Lewis</a> tried for years to get his hands on the part of Holden."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayn_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-mayn-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger repeatedly refused, though, and in 1999, Joyce Maynard definitively concluded: "The only person who might ever have played Holden Caulfield would have been J. D. Salinger."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayn_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-mayn-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_in_the_1950s_and_move_to_Cornish">Writing in the 1950s and move to Cornish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Writing in the 1950s and move to Cornish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In a July 1951 profile in <i>Book of the Month Club News</i>, Salinger's friend and <i>New Yorker</i> editor <a href="/wiki/William_Keepers_Maxwell,_Jr." title="William Keepers Maxwell, Jr.">William Maxwell</a> asked Salinger about his literary influences. Salinger responded: "A writer, when he's asked to discuss his craft, ought to get up and call out in a loud voice just the names of the writers he loves. I love <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Kafka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">Flaubert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Tolstoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Chekhov" title="Anton Chekhov">Chekhov</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoevsky">Dostoevsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Proust</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sean_O%27Casey" title="Sean O'Casey">O'Casey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Maria_Rilke" title="Rainer Maria Rilke">Rilke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">Lorca</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">Keats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">Rimbaud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Burns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Bront%C3%AB" title="Emily Brontë">E. Brontë</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Coleridge</a>. I won't name any living writers. I don't think it's right."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> In letters written in the 1940s, Salinger had expressed his admiration of three living, or recently deceased, writers: <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Anderson" title="Sherwood Anderson">Sherwood Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ring_Lardner" title="Ring Lardner">Ring Lardner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F. Scott Fitzgerald</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-influ_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-influ-72"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Ian Hamilton wrote that Salinger even saw himself for some time as "Fitzgerald's successor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's "<a href="/wiki/A_Perfect_Day_for_Bananafish" title="A Perfect Day for Bananafish">A Perfect Day for Bananafish</a>" has an ending similar to that of Fitzgerald's earlier published short story "<a href="/wiki/May_Day_(short_story)" title="May Day (short story)">May Day</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After several years of practicing Zen Buddhism, in 1952, while reading <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gospel_of_Sri_Ramakrishna" title="The Gospel of Sri Ramakrishna">The Gospel of Sri Ramakrishna</a></i> about <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> religious teacher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Ramakrishna" title="Sri Ramakrishna">Sri Ramakrishna</a>, Salinger wrote friends of a momentous change in his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> He became an adherent of Ramakrishna's <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> Hinduism, which advocated celibacy for those seeking enlightenment, and detachment from human responsibilities such as family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vive_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-vive-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's religious studies were reflected in some of his writing. The story "<a href="/wiki/Teddy_(story)" title="Teddy (story)">Teddy</a>" features a ten-year-old child who expresses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedantic" title="Vedantic">Vedantic</a> insights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> He also studied <a href="/wiki/Bibliography_of_Swami_Vivekananda" title="Bibliography of Swami Vivekananda">the writings</a> of Ramakrishna's disciple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vivekananda" title="Vivekananda">Vivekananda</a>; in the story "<a href="/wiki/Hapworth_16,_1924" title="Hapworth 16, 1924">Hapworth 16, 1924</a>", the character of Seymour Glass describes him as "one of the most exciting, original and best-equipped giants of this century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vive_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-vive-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1953, Salinger published a collection of seven stories from <i>The New Yorker</i> ("Bananafish" among them), as well as two that the magazine had rejected. The collection was published as <i><a href="/wiki/Nine_Stories_(Salinger)" title="Nine Stories (Salinger)">Nine Stories</a></i> in the United States, and "<a href="/wiki/For_Esm%C3%A9%E2%80%94with_Love_and_Squalor" title="For Esmé—with Love and Squalor">For Esmé—with Love and Squalor</a>" in the UK, after one of Salinger's best-known stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The book received grudgingly positive reviews, and was a financial success—"remarkably so for a volume of short stories", according to Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niney_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-niney-80"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Nine Stories</i> spent three months on the <i>New York Times</i> Bestseller list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niney_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-niney-80"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Already tightening his grip on publicity, though, Salinger refused to allow publishers of the collection to depict his characters in dust jacket illustrations, lest readers form preconceived notions of them.</p>
<p>As the notoriety of <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> grew, Salinger gradually withdrew from public view. In 1953, he moved from an apartment at <a href="/wiki/300_East_57th_Street" title="300 East 57th Street">300 East 57th Street</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> New York, to <a href="/wiki/Cornish,_New_Hampshire" title="Cornish, New Hampshire">Cornish, New Hampshire</a>. Early in his time at Cornish he was relatively sociable, particularly with students at Windsor High School. Salinger invited them to his house frequently to play records and talk about problems at school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twelve_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-twelve-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> One such student, Shirley Blaney, persuaded Salinger to be interviewed for the high school page of <i>The Daily Eagle</i>, the city paper. Nonetheless, after Blaney's interview appeared prominently in the newspaper's editorial section, Salinger cut off all contact with the high schoolers without explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twelve_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-twelve-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also seen less frequently around town, meeting only one close friend—jurist <a href="/wiki/Learned_Hand" title="Learned Hand">Learned Hand</a>—with any regularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> He also began to publish with less frequency. After the 1953 publication of <i>Nine Stories</i>, he published only four stories through the rest of the decade; two in 1955 and one each in 1957 and 1959.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Marriage and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In June 1955, at the age of 36, Salinger married Claire Douglas, a <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe</a> student (her father was the art critic <a href="/wiki/Robert_Langton_Douglas" title="Robert Langton Douglas">Robert Langton Douglas</a>). They had two children, Margaret (b. December 10, 1955) and <a href="/wiki/Matt_Salinger" title="Matt Salinger">Matthew</a> (b. February 13, 1960). Margaret Salinger wrote in her memoir <i>Dream Catcher</i> that she believes her parents would not have married, nor would she have been born, had her father not read the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Lahiri_Mahasaya" title="Lahiri Mahasaya">Lahiri Mahasaya</a>, a guru of <a href="/wiki/Paramahansa_Yogananda" title="Paramahansa Yogananda">Paramahansa Yogananda</a>, which brought the possibility of enlightenment to those following the path of the "householder" (a married person with children).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eiga_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-eiga-84"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> After their marriage, J.D. and Claire were initiated into the path of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kriya_yoga" title="Kriya yoga">Kriya yoga</a> in a small store-front Hindu temple in Washington, D.C., during the summer of 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nine_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-nine-85"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> They received a mantra and breathing exercises to practice for ten minutes twice a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nine_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-nine-85"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Salinger also insisted that Claire drop out of school and live with him, only four months shy of graduation, which she did. Certain elements of the story "Franny", published in January 1955, are based on his relationship with Claire, including her ownership of the book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Way_of_the_Pilgrim" title="The Way of the Pilgrim">The Way of the Pilgrim</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Because of their isolated location and Salinger's proclivities, they hardly saw other people for long stretches of time. Claire was also frustrated by Salinger's ever-changing religious beliefs. Though she committed herself to Kriya yoga, she remembered that Salinger would chronically leave Cornish to work on a story "for several weeks only to return with the piece he was supposed to be finishing all undone or destroyed and some new 'ism' we had to follow."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salinger94_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salinger94-87"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Claire believed "it was to cover the fact that Jerry had just destroyed or junked or couldn't face the quality of, or couldn't face publishing, what he had created."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salinger94_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salinger94-87"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After abandoning Kriya yoga, Salinger tried <a href="/wiki/Dianetics" title="Dianetics">Dianetics</a> (the forerunner of <a href="/wiki/Scientology" title="Scientology">Scientology</a>), even meeting its founder <a href="/wiki/L._Ron_Hubbard" title="L. Ron Hubbard">L. Ron Hubbard</a>, but according to Claire he was quickly disenchanted with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salinger94_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Salinger94-87"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> This was followed by an adherence to a number of spiritual, medical, and nutritional belief systems including an interest in <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Cayce" title="Edgar Cayce">Edgar Cayce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrobiotics" title="Macrobiotics">macrobiotics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salinger.2C_M_2000._p._195_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salinger.2C_M_2000._p._195-89"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Salinger's family life was further marked by discord after the first child was born; according to Margaret's book, Claire felt that her daughter had replaced her in Salinger's affections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The infant Margaret was sick much of the time, but Salinger, having embraced the tenets of Christian Science, refused to take her to a doctor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sixteen_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-sixteen-91"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Margaret, her mother admitted to her years later that she went "over the edge" in the winter of 1957 and had made plans to murder her and then commit suicide. Claire had supposedly intended to do it during a trip to New York City with Salinger, but she instead acted on a sudden impulse to take Margaret from the hotel and run away. After a few months, Salinger persuaded her to return to Cornish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sixteen_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-sixteen-91"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_publications_and_Maynard_relationship">Last publications and Maynard relationship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Last publications and Maynard relationship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Salinger published <i><a href="/wiki/Franny_and_Zooey" title="Franny and Zooey">Franny and Zooey</a></i> in 1961, and <i><a href="/wiki/Raise_High_the_Roof_Beam,_Carpenters_and_Seymour:_An_Introduction" title="Raise High the Roof Beam, Carpenters and Seymour: An Introduction">Raise High the Roof Beam, Carpenters and Seymour: An Introduction</a></i> in 1963. Each book contained two short stories or novellas, previously published in <i>The New Yorker</i>, about members of the Glass family. These four stories were originally published between 1955 and 1959, and were the only ones Salinger had published since <i>Nine Stories</i>. On the dust jacket of <i>Franny and Zooey</i>, Salinger wrote, in reference to his interest in privacy: "It is my rather subversive opinion that a writer's feelings of anonymity-obscurity are the second most valuable property on loan to him during his working years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On September 15, 1961, <i>Time</i> magazine devoted its cover to Salinger. In an article that profiled his "life of recluse", the magazine reported that the Glass family series "is nowhere near completion ... Salinger intends to write a Glass trilogy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sonny_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-sonny-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, Salinger published only one other story after that: "<a href="/wiki/Hapworth_16,_1924" title="Hapworth 16, 1924">Hapworth 16, 1924</a>", a novella in the form of a long letter from seven-year-old Seymour Glass while at summer camp. His first new work in six years, the novella took up most of the June 19, 1965, issue of <i>The New Yorker,</i> and was universally critically panned. Around this time, Salinger had isolated Claire from friends and relatives and made her—in the words of Margaret Salinger—"a virtual prisoner".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salinger94_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-Salinger94-87"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Claire separated from him in September 1966; their divorce was finalized on October 3, 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1972, at the age of 53, Salinger had a relationship with 18-year-old <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Maynard" title="Joyce Maynard">Joyce Maynard</a> that lasted for nine months. Maynard, at this time, was already an experienced writer for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seventeen_(magazine)" title="Seventeen (magazine)">Seventeen</a></i> magazine. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> had asked Maynard to write an article for them which, when published as "An Eighteen-Year-Old Looks Back On Life" on April 23, 1972,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> made her a celebrity. Salinger wrote a letter to her warning about living with fame. After exchanging 25 letters, Maynard moved in with Salinger the summer after her freshman year at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-women_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-women-95"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Maynard did not return to Yale that fall, and spent ten months as a guest in Salinger's Cornish home. The relationship ended, he told his daughter Margaret at a family outing, because Maynard wanted children, and he felt he was too old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, in her own autobiography, Maynard paints a different picture, saying Salinger abruptly ended the relationship and refused to take her back. She had dropped out of Yale to be with him, even forgoing a scholarship. Maynard later writes in her own memoir how she came to find out that Salinger had begun relationships with young women by exchanging letters. One of those letter recipients included Salinger's last wife, a nurse who was already engaged to be married to someone else when she met the author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While he was living with Maynard, Salinger continued to write in a disciplined fashion, a few hours every morning. According to Maynard, by 1972 he had completed two new novels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> In a rare 1974 interview with <i>The New York Times</i>, he explained: "There is a marvelous peace in not publishing ... I like to write. I love to write. But I write just for myself and my own pleasure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Maynard, he saw publication as "a damned interruption".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-damned_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-damned-101"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> In her memoir, Margaret Salinger describes the detailed filing system her father had for his unpublished manuscripts: "A red mark meant, if I die before I finish my work, publish this 'as is,' blue meant publish but edit first, and so on."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> A neighbor said that Salinger told him that he had written 15 unpublished novels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Salinger's final interview was in June 1980 with Betty Eppes of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Advocate_(Louisiana)" title="The Advocate (Louisiana)">The Baton Rouge Advocate</a></i>, which has been represented somewhat differently, depending on the secondary source. By one account, Eppes was an attractive young woman who misrepresented herself as an aspiring novelist, and managed to record audio of the interview as well as take several photographs of Salinger, both without his knowledge or consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> In a separate account, emphasis is placed on her contact by letter writing from the local Post Office, and Salinger's personal initiative to cross the bridge to meet with the woman, who in the course of the interview made clear she was a reporter (and who did indeed, at the close, take pictures of Salinger as he departed).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-105"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the first account, the interview ended "disastrously" when a local passer-by from Cornish attempted to shake the famous author's hand, at which point Salinger became enraged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> A further account of the interview published later in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Paris_Review" title="The Paris Review">The Paris Review</a></i>, purportedly by Eppes as author, has been disowned by Eppes and separately ascribed as a derived work of Review Editor <a href="/wiki/George_Plimpton" title="George Plimpton">George Plimpton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-105"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_conflicts">Legal conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Legal conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although Salinger tried to escape public exposure as much as possible, he continued to struggle with unwanted attention from both the media and the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Readers of his work and students from nearby <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a> often came to Cornish in groups, hoping to catch a glimpse of him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1986 Salinger learned that the British writer <a href="/wiki/Ian_Hamilton_(critic)" title="Ian Hamilton (critic)">Ian Hamilton</a> intended to publish a biography that made extensive use of letters Salinger had written to other authors and friends. Salinger sued to stop the book's publication. The court in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salinger_v._Random_House" title="Salinger v. Random House">Salinger v. Random House</a></i> ruled that Hamilton's extensive use of the letters, including quotation and paraphrasing, was not acceptable since the author's right to control publication overrode the right of fair use. The book was not published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lubas_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-lubas-112"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Later, Hamilton published <i>In Search of J.D. Salinger: A Writing Life (1935–65)</i>, but this book was more about his experience in tracking down information and the copyright fights over the planned biography than about Salinger himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unintended_consequence" title="Unintended consequence">unintended consequence</a> of the lawsuit was that many details of Salinger's private life, including that he had spent the last twenty years writing, in his words, "Just a work of fiction ... That's all",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-depos_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-depos-52"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> became public in the form of court transcripts. Excerpts from his letters were also widely disseminated, most notably a bitter remark written in response to <a href="/wiki/Oona_O%27Neill" title="Oona O'Neill">Oona O'Neill</a>'s marriage to <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I can see them at home evenings. Chaplin squatting grey and nude, atop his <a href="/wiki/Chiffonier" title="Chiffonier">chiffonier</a>, swinging his thyroid around his head by his bamboo cane, like a dead rat. Oona in an aquamarine gown, applauding madly from the bathroom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trespass_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-trespass-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lubas_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-lubas-112"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Salinger was romantically involved with television actress <a href="/wiki/Elaine_Joyce" title="Elaine Joyce">Elaine Joyce</a> for several years in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-women_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-women-95"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The relationship ended when he met Colleen O'Neill (b. June 11, 1959), a nurse and quiltmaker, whom he married around 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> O'Neill, forty years his junior, once told Margaret Salinger that she and Salinger were trying to have a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Iran" title="Cinema of Iran">Iranian director</a> <a href="/wiki/Dariush_Mehrjui" title="Dariush Mehrjui">Dariush Mehrjui</a> released the film <i><a href="/wiki/Pari_(film)" title="Pari (film)">Pari</a></i>, an unauthorized and loose adaptation of Salinger's <i>Franny and Zooey</i>. Though the film could be distributed legally in Iran since the country has no official copyright relations with the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger had his lawyers block a planned screening of the film at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_Center" title="Lincoln Center">Lincoln Center</a> in 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mehr_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-mehr-117"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Mehrjui called Salinger's action "bewildering", explaining that he saw his film as "a kind of cultural exchange".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-118"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, Salinger gave a small publisher, Orchises Press, permission to publish "<a href="/wiki/Hapworth_16,_1924" title="Hapworth 16, 1924">Hapworth 16, 1924</a>", the previously uncollected novella.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> It was to be published that year, and listings for it appeared at <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a> and other book-sellers. After a flurry of articles and critical reviews of the story appeared in the press, the publication date was pushed back repeatedly before apparently being cancelled altogether. Amazon anticipated that Orchises would publish the story in January 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orchise_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-orchise-120"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> but at the time of his death it was still listed as "currently unavailable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2009, Salinger consulted lawyers about the upcoming publication in the US of an unauthorized sequel to <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> written by Swedish book publisher Fredrik Colting under the pseudonym J. D. California. California's book is called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/60_Years_Later:_Coming_Through_the_Rye" title="60 Years Later: Coming Through the Rye">60 Years Later: Coming Through the Rye</a></i>, and appears to pick up the story of Salinger's protagonist Holden Caulfield. In Salinger's novel, Caulfield is 17 years old, wandering the streets of New York after being expelled from his private school; the California book features a 76-year-old man, "Mr. C", musing on having escaped his nursing home. Salinger's New York literary agent Phyllis Westberg told Britain's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunday_Telegraph" title="Sunday Telegraph">Sunday Telegraph</a></i>: "The matter has been turned over to a lawyer." The fact that little was known about Colting and the book was set to be published by a new publishing imprint called 'Windupbird Publishing' gave rise to speculation in literary circles that the whole thing might be a stunt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> District court judge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deborah_A._Batts" title="Deborah A. Batts">Deborah A. Batts</a> issued an <a href="/wiki/Injunction" title="Injunction">injunction</a> which prevents the book from being published within the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> The book's author filed an appeal on July 23, 2009; it was heard in the Second Circuit Court of Appeals on September 3, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> The case was settled in 2011 when Colting agreed not to publish or otherwise distribute the book, e-book, or any other editions of <i>60 Years Later</i> in the U.S. or Canada until <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> enters the public domain, while also refraining from using the title "Coming through the Rye", dedicating the book to Salinger or referring to the title "The Catcher in the Rye", while Colting remains free to sell the book in other international territories without fear of interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_publicity">Later publicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Later publicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1999, 25 years after the end of their relationship, <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Maynard" title="Joyce Maynard">Joyce Maynard</a> put up for auction a series of letters Salinger had written to her. Maynard's memoir of her life and her relationship with Salinger, <i>At Home in the World: A Memoir</i>, was published the same year. Among other topics, the book described how Maynard's mother had consulted with her on how to appeal to the aging author (dressing like a child), and described Joyce's relationship with him at length. In the ensuing controversy over both the memoir and the letters, Maynard claimed that she was forced to auction the letters for financial reasons; she would have preferred to donate them to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beinecke_Library" title="Beinecke Library">Beinecke Library</a>. Software developer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Norton" title="Peter Norton">Peter Norton</a> bought the letters for $156,500 and announced his intention to return them to Salinger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auction_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-auction-129"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dream_Catcher_(memoir).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="250" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/26/Dream_Catcher_%28memoir%29.jpg/170px-Dream_Catcher_%28memoir%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/26/Dream_Catcher_%28memoir%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/26/Dream_Catcher_%28memoir%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dream_Catcher_(memoir).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Margaret Salinger's memoir <i>Dream Catcher</i>, its cover featuring a rare photograph of Salinger</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A year later, Salinger's daughter Margaret by his second wife Claire Douglas, published <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dream_Catcher:_A_Memoir&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dream Catcher: A Memoir (page does not exist)">Dream Catcher: A Memoir</a></i>. In her book, she described the harrowing control Salinger had over her mother and dispelled many of the Salinger myths established by Ian Hamilton's book. One of Hamilton's arguments was that Salinger's experience with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a> left him psychologically scarred, and that he was unable to deal with the traumatic nature of his war service. Though Ms. Salinger allowed that "the few men who lived through '<a href="/wiki/Operation_L%C3%BCttich" title="Operation Lüttich">Bloody Mortain</a>,' a battle in which her father fought, were left with much to sicken them, body and soul",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fiff_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-fiff-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> she also painted a picture of her father as a man immensely proud of his service record, maintaining his military haircut and service jacket, and moving about his compound (and town) in an old <a href="/wiki/Jeep" title="Jeep">Jeep</a>.</p>
<p>Both Margaret Salinger and Maynard characterized the author as a devoted film buff. According to Margaret, his favorite movies include <i><a href="/wiki/Gigi_(1958_film)" title="Gigi (1958 film)">Gigi</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lady_Vanishes_(1938_film)" title="The Lady Vanishes (1938 film)">The Lady Vanishes</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_39_Steps_(1935_film)" title="The 39 Steps (1935 film)">The 39 Steps</a></i> (Phoebe's favorite movie in <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>), and the comedies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W.C._Fields" title="W.C. Fields">W.C. Fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurel_and_Hardy" title="Laurel and Hardy">Laurel and Hardy</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marx_Bros." title="Marx Bros.">Marx Bros.</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Predating VCRs, Salinger had an extensive collection of classic movies from the 1940s in 16 mm prints. Maynard wrote that "he loves movies, not films," <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> and his daughter argued that her father's "worldview is, essentially, a product of the movies of his day. To my father, all Spanish speakers are <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_people" title="Puerto Rican people">Puerto Rican</a> washerwomen, or the toothless, grinning-gypsy types in a Marx Brothers movie."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salinger.2C_M_2000._p._195_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salinger.2C_M_2000._p._195-89"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Ross_(journalist)" title="Lillian Ross (journalist)">Lillian Ross</a>, a staff writer for <i>The New Yorker</i> and longtime friend of Salinger, wrote following his death, "Salinger loved movies, and he was more fun than anyone to discuss them with. He enjoyed watching actors work, and he enjoyed knowing them. (He loved <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bancroft" title="Anne Bancroft">Anne Bancroft</a>, hated <a href="/wiki/Audrey_Hepburn" title="Audrey Hepburn">Audrey Hepburn</a>, and said that he had seen <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Illusion_(film)" title="Grand Illusion (film)">Grand Illusion</a></i> ten times.)"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross2010_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ross2010-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Margaret also offered many insights into other Salinger myths, including her father's supposed long-time interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrobiotics" title="Macrobiotics">macrobiotics</a> and involvement with "alternative medicine" and Eastern philosophies. A few weeks after <i>Dream Catcher</i> was published, Margaret's brother <a href="/wiki/Matt_Salinger" title="Matt Salinger">Matt</a> discredited the memoir in a letter to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Observer" title="The New York Observer">The New York Observer</a></i>. He disparaged his sister's "gothic tales of our supposed childhood" and stated: "I can't say with any authority that she is consciously making anything up. I just know that I grew up in a very different house, with two very different parents from those my sister describes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justice_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-justice-132"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Salinger died of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_causes" title="Natural causes">natural causes</a> at his home in <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> on January 27, 2010.
