He was 91.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeathABC_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeathABC-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's literary representative told <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> that the writer had broken his <a href="/wiki/Hip_bone" title="Hip bone">hip</a> in May 2009, but that "his health had been excellent until a rather sudden decline after the new year."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt12810_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt12810-133"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> The representative believed that Salinger's death was not a painful one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt12810_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt12810-133"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> His third wife and widow, Colleen O'Neill Zakrzeski Salinger, and Salinger's son Matt became the executors of his estate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt12810_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt12810-133"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_style_and_themes">Literary style and themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Literary style and themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In a contributor's note Salinger gave to <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper's Magazine">Harper's Magazine</a></i> in 1946, he wrote: "I almost always write about very young people", a statement that has been referred to as his <a href="/wiki/Credo" title="Credo">credo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Adolescents are featured or appear in all of Salinger's work, from his first published short story, "The Young Folks", to <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> and his <a href="/wiki/Glass_family" title="Glass family">Glass family</a> stories. In 1961, the critic <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Kazin" title="Alfred Kazin">Alfred Kazin</a> explained that Salinger's choice of teenagers as a subject matter was one reason for his appeal to young readers, but another was "a consciousness [among youths] that he speaks for them and virtually <i>to</i> them, in a language that is peculiarly honest and their own, with a vision of things that capture their most secret judgments of the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> For this reason, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Mailer" title="Norman Mailer">Norman Mailer</a> once remarked that Salinger was "the greatest mind ever to stay in prep school".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Salinger's language, especially his energetic, realistically sparse dialogue, was revolutionary at the time his first stories were published, and was seen by several critics as "the most distinguishing thing" about his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Salinger identified closely with his characters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-damned_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-damned-101"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> and used techniques such as interior monologue, letters, and extended telephone calls to display his gift for dialogue. Such style elements also "[gave] him the illusion of having, as it were, delivered his characters' destinies into their own keeping."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> Recurring themes in Salinger's stories also connect to the ideas of innocence and adolescence, including the "corrupting influence of Hollywood and the world at large",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mondy_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-mondy-139"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> the disconnect between teenagers and "phony" adults,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mondy_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-mondy-139"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> and the perceptive, precocious intelligence of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-at_fifty_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-at_fifty-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Contemporary critics discuss a clear progression over the course of Salinger's published work, as evidenced by the increasingly negative reviews received by each of his three post-<i>Catcher</i> story collections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justice_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-justice-132"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> Ian Hamilton adheres to this view, arguing that while Salinger's early stories for the "slicks" boasted "tight, energetic" dialogue, they had also been formulaic and sentimental. It took the standards of <i>The New Yorker</i> editors, among them <a href="/wiki/William_Shawn" title="William Shawn">William Shawn</a>, to refine his writing into the "spare, teasingly mysterious, withheld" qualities of "A Perfect Day for Bananafish", <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>, and his stories of the early 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> By the late 1950s, as Salinger became more reclusive and involved in religious study, Hamilton notes that his stories became longer, less plot-driven, and increasingly filled with <a href="/wiki/Digression" title="Digression">digression</a> and parenthetical remarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Menand" title="Louis Menand">Louis Menand</a> agrees, writing in <i>The New Yorker</i> that Salinger "stopped writing stories, in the conventional sense ... He seemed to lose interest in fiction as an art form—perhaps he thought there was something manipulative or inauthentic about literary device and authorial control."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-at_fifty_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-at_fifty-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> In recent years, some critics have defended certain post-<i>Nine Stories</i> works by Salinger; in 2001, <a href="/wiki/Janet_Malcolm" title="Janet Malcolm">Janet Malcolm</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">The New York Review of Books</a></i> that "Zooey" "is arguably Salinger's masterpiece ... Rereading it and its companion piece "Franny" is no less rewarding than rereading <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Gatsby" title="The Great Gatsby">The Great Gatsby</a></i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justice_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-justice-132"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Salinger's writing has influenced several prominent writers, prompting <a href="/wiki/Harold_Brodkey" title="Harold Brodkey">Harold Brodkey</a> (himself an <a href="/wiki/O._Henry_Award" title="O. Henry Award">O. Henry Award</a>-winning author) to state in 1991: "His is the most influential body of work in English prose by anyone since Hemingway."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> Of the writers in Salinger's generation, <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>-winning novelist <a href="/wiki/John_Updike" title="John Updike">John Updike</a> attested that "the short stories of J. D. Salinger really opened my eyes as to how you can weave fiction out of a set of events that seem almost unconnected, or very lightly connected ... [Reading Salinger] stick[s] in my mind as really having moved me a step up, as it were, toward knowing how to handle my own material."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> The critic <a href="/wiki/Louis_Menand" title="Louis Menand">Louis Menand</a> has observed that the early stories of Pulitzer Prize-winner <a href="/wiki/Philip_Roth" title="Philip Roth">Philip Roth</a> were affected by "Salinger's voice and comic timing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-at_fifty_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-at_fifty-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/National_Book_Award" title="National Book Award">National Book Award</a> finalist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Yates_(novelist)" title="Richard Yates (novelist)">Richard Yates</a> told <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> in 1977 that reading Salinger's stories for the first time was a landmark experience, and that "nothing quite like it has happened to me since".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> Yates describes Salinger as "a man who used language as if it were pure energy beautifully controlled, and who knew exactly what he was doing in every silence as well as in every word." <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Lish" title="Gordon Lish">Gordon Lish</a>'s O. Henry Award-winning short story "For Jeromé—With Love and Kisses" (1977, collected in <i>What I Know So Far</i>, 1984), is a parody of Salinger's "For Esmé—with Love and Squalor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2001, Louis Menand wrote in <i>The New Yorker</i> that "<i>Catcher in the Rye</i> rewrites" among each new generation had become "a literary genre all its own".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-at_fifty_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-at_fifty-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> He classed among them <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Plath" title="Sylvia Plath">Sylvia Plath</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bell_Jar" title="The Bell Jar">The Bell Jar</a></i> (1963), <a href="/wiki/Hunter_S._Thompson" title="Hunter S. Thompson">Hunter S. Thompson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Fear_and_Loathing_in_Las_Vegas" title="Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas">Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas</a></i> (1971), <a href="/wiki/Jay_McInerney" title="Jay McInerney">Jay McInerney</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Bright_Lights,_Big_City_(novel)" title="Bright Lights, Big City (novel)">Bright Lights, Big City</a></i> (1984), and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Eggers" title="Dave Eggers">Dave Eggers</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Heartbreaking_Work_of_Staggering_Genius" title="A Heartbreaking Work of Staggering Genius">A Heartbreaking Work of Staggering Genius</a></i> (2000). Writer <a href="/wiki/Aimee_Bender" title="Aimee Bender">Aimee Bender</a> was struggling with her first short stories when a friend gave her a copy of <i>Nine Stories</i>; inspired, she later described Salinger's effect on writers, explaining: "[I]t feels like Salinger wrote <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i> in a day, and that incredible feeling of ease inspires writing. Inspires the pursuit of voice. Not his voice. My voice. Your voice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Authors such as <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Chbosky" title="Stephen Chbosky">Stephen Chbosky</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Safran_Foer" title="Jonathan Safran Foer">Jonathan Safran Foer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Hiaasen" title="Carl Hiaasen">Carl Hiaasen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susan_Minot" title="Susan Minot">Susan Minot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minot_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-minot-150"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami">Haruki Murakami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gwendoline_Riley" title="Gwendoline Riley">Gwendoline Riley</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tom_Robbins" title="Tom Robbins">Tom Robbins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sachar" title="Louis Sachar">Louis Sachar</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joel_Stein" title="Joel Stein">Joel Stein</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Green_(author)" title="John Green (author)">John Green</a> have cited Salinger as an influence. Musician <a href="/wiki/Tomas_Kalnoky" title="Tomas Kalnoky">Tomas Kalnoky</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streetlight_manifesto" title="Streetlight manifesto">Streetlight Manifesto</a> also cites Salinger as an influence, referencing him and <a href="/wiki/Holden_Caulfield" title="Holden Caulfield">Holden Caulfield</a>, the main character of <i>Catcher in the Rye</i>, in the song "<a href="/wiki/Everything_Goes_Numb#Cultural_references" title="Everything Goes Numb">Here's To Life</a>". Biographer Paul Alexander called Salinger "the <a href="/wiki/Greta_Garbo" title="Greta Garbo">Greta Garbo</a> of literature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1960s, J. D. Salinger was himself drawn to <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi mysticism</a> through the writer and thinker <a href="/wiki/Idries_Shah" title="Idries Shah">Idries Shah</a>'s seminal work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sufis" title="The Sufis">The Sufis</a></i>, as were others writers such as <a href="/wiki/Doris_Lessing" title="Doris Lessing">Doris Lessing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Grigson" title="Geoffrey Grigson">Geoffrey Grigson</a>, and the poets <a href="/wiki/Robert_Graves" title="Robert Graves">Robert Graves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Hughes" title="Ted Hughes">Ted Hughes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sufism-Guardian-Webster_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sufism-Guardian-Webster-155"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> As well as Idries Shah, Salinger also read the Taoist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Lao Tse</a> and the Hindu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vivekananda" title="Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a> who introduced the Indian philosophies of Vedanta and Yoga to the Western world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Istorie_a_Amantelor_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Istorie_a_Amantelor-156"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In a biography titled <i>Salinger</i>, authors <a href="/wiki/David_Shields" title="David Shields">David Shields</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shane_Salerno" title="Shane Salerno">Shane Salerno</a> assert that the author had left specific instructions authorizing a timetable, to start between 2015 and 2020, for the release of several unpublished works. According to the authors and their sources, these include five new Glass-family stories; a novel based on Salinger's relationship with his first wife, Sylvia; a novella in the form of a WWII counterintelligence officer’s diary; a "manual" of stories about Vedanta; and other new or retooled stories that illuminate the life of Holden Caulfield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shields_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shields-157"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Salinger</i> biography is also described as a companion volume to <a href="/wiki/Salinger_(film)" title="Salinger (film)">a film documentary of the same title</a>. The directorial debut of writer <a href="/wiki/Shane_Salerno" title="Shane Salerno">Shane Salerno</a>, <i>Salinger</i> was made over nine years and received a limited theatrical release on September 6, 2013 with distribution by <a href="/wiki/The_Weinstein_Company" title="The Weinstein Company">The Weinstein Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-158"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMDb_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMDb-159"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Published_and_anthologized_stories">Published and anthologized stories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Published and anthologized stories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=J._D._Salinger&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><span class="citation journal" id="CITEREFWhitfield1997">Whitfield, Stephen (December 1997). <a class="external text" href="http://www.northeastern.edu/neq/pdfs/Whitfield%2C_Stephen_J.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Cherished and Cursed: Toward a Social History of The Catcher in the Rye"</a> (PDF). <i>The New England Quarterly</i> <b>70</b> (4): 567–600. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.2307%2F366646" rel="nofollow">10.2307/366646</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/366646" rel="nofollow">366646</a><span class="reference-accessdate">.
